Jamison Brereton

101 (813)
Soma Pavama╠Дna
Andh─лgu ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыvi (1тАУ3), Yay─Бti N─Бhuс╣гa (4тАУ6), Nahuс╣гa M─Бnava (7тАУ9), Manu S─Бс╣Гvaraс╣Зa (10тАУ12), Praj─Бpati (13тАУ16)
16 verses: anuс╣гс╣нubh, except g─Бyatr─л 2тАУ3, arranged in trcas ╠е
In order to conform to the pattern of arrangement, this last hymn in the anuс╣гс╣нubh group must actually have consisted originally of five three-verse hymns, with a single verse (16) appended, as was already pointed out by Oldenberg (1888: 201). There is, however, some interconnection among the tr╠еcas that points to a sense of cohesion. In particular, the first and last tr╠еcas (vss. 1тАУ3 and 13тАУ15) are linked by the striking image in their first verses (1, 13) of a sacrifice-defiling dog drawn to the sound of the soma being pressed, a dog that must be driven away. Verse 13 also contains a brief and enigmatic allusion to a myth also enigmatically mentioned to in X.171.2.
Most of the rest of the hymn contains standard material: the details of prepara tion of the drink and its journey across the filter to mix with the milk, as well as its relationship to the gods, especially Indra. There is some internal unity visible in the individual tr╠еcas, but it is not pronounced.

Jamison Brereton Notes

On the division of this hymn into tс╣Ыcas and the structure of the hymn in general see published introduction. The Anukramaс╣З─л assigns each tс╣Ыca to a different poet, in roughly reverse (conceptual) chronological order. The last tс╣Ыca plus appended vs., vss. 13-16, is credited to Praj─Бpati, the Middle Vedic creator god, with no patronymic. The poet of the 2nd to last tс╣Ыca, vss. 10-12, is given as Manu S─Бс╣Гvaraс╣Зa. This poet is in fact referred to, with the patryonymic S─Бс╣Гvaraс╣Зi, in the first V─Бlakhilya hymn, VIII.51.1 y├бth─Б m├бnau s─Бс╣Гvaraс╣Зau, s├│mam indr─Б╠Бpibaс╕е sut├бm тАЬJust as at Manu S─Бс╣Гvaraс╣Зi’s you drank pressed soma, Indra тАжтАЭ But even if the reference is just to a revered ancient poet, the name Manu, as the ur-man and ursacrificer, resonates in the context of Praj─Бpati. Moreover, thereтАЩs a missing step: the Anukr. attributes V.33-34 to one Saс╣Гvaraс╣Зa Pr─Бj─Бpatya, who would be the gapped generational link between Praj─Бpati and Manu S─Бс╣Гvaraс╣Зa (/i). The names of the next two poets display the proper generational relationship: the third tс╣Ыca from the end (vss. 7-9) is by Nahuс╣гa M─Бnava, with his patronymic from Manu; the fourth tс╣Ыca from the end (vss. 4-6) by Yay─Бti N─Бhuс╣гa, again taking his patronymic from the next poet in order. The first tс╣Ыca (vss. 1-3) does not participate in this generational chain; it is attributed to Andh─лgu ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыvi, a patronymic that links him to the skilled poet of the Marut hymns of V (52-61), ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva ─Аtreya (for further on this attribution see comm. ad vs. 1 below). Leaving Andh─лgu aside, it seems that the Anukr. takes the hymn from a presumably contemporary poet Yay─Бti back through the ages (and 4-5 generations) to the primal god Praj─Бpati, whose primacy is signaled by his lack of patronymic.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬFor (the drinking of) the effused exhilarating (juice) of the Soma who has plural ced victory before you, do you friends destroy the long-tongued dog.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

I.e., prevent the dogs or r─Бkс╣гasas from lapping the Soma

Jamison Brereton

With your advance victory over the stalk, for the pressed soma to cause exhilaration,
pierce away the dog that has the long tongue, o comrades.

Jamison Brereton Notes

As noted in the published introduction. the appearance of the sacrifice-defiling dog in this vs. and vs. 13 sketches a ring. The content of the vs. and its reason for inclusion here are puzzling. Our long-tongued dog (acc. ┼Ыv─Б╠Бnam тАж d─лrghajihvy├аm) is clearly connected with a story widely attested in Vedic prose of an female demon, an Asur─л called тАЬLong-tonguedтАЭ (d─лrghajihv─л╠Б), who licks (and thus defiles) the sacrifice. The story is found in texts belonging to all three ritual Vedas: RV: AB II.22; YV: MS III.10.6, KS XXIX.1; SV: JB I.161-63, PB XIII.6.9-10, though it is most developed in the JB, where it takes a distinctly and entertainingly sexual turn. The story is treated extensively by Oertel in a number of publs. (see reff. in OтАЩFlaherty, JB, pp.

124-25) and tr. by Caland in JB in Auswahl and his ed. of PB (incl. an Engl. tr. of the JB version ad PB XIII.6.10); see also W. D. OтАЩFlaherty, Tales of Sex and Violence: Folklore, Sacrifice, and Danger in the Jaimin─лya Br─Бhmaс╣Зa (1985), 100-103.

Whether the long-tongued demoness of prose is identical with or was inspired by our long-tongued dog is unclear, but at least the JB connects its account, perhaps secondarily, with our vs. After Indra set a certain Sumitra to seduce the demoness and get her into his power so that Indra could slay her, Sumitra calls upon Indra with our vs. (quoted in the JB text I.162), which Indra then makes into his vajra, raises as his weapon (et─Бm anuс╣гс╣нubhaс╣Г vajram udyatya), and smites her. The same vs. then figures in the immediately following story (I.162-63), in which ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva, rather nastily tricked by his Sattra mates, recites the verse (now named the ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva s─Бman), to get himself to heaven. And this story involving ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva himself is followed soon after (JB I.165) by one whose main character is Andh─лgu (see also PB VIII.5.8-12). So at least serially Andh─лgu and ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva connect to D─лrghajihv─л - remember that the poet of this tс╣Ыca is given by the Anukr. as Andh─лgu ┼Ъy─Бv─Б┼Ыva.

Unfortunately nothing in the prose narratives provides any help in interpreting our vs., esp. the hapax cmpd. pur├│jiti-. Both Geldner and Renou take the instr. pur├│jit─л as expressing purpose: тАЬauf dass euer Trank zuv├╢rderst siegeтАЭ; тАЬafin qu’il y ait victoire de votre jus.тАЭ I do not understand the case syntax of this (instr. of purpose?), esp. as the standard dative of purpose appears in the next p─Бda (sut─Б╠Бya m─Бdayitn├бve), and, with the omission of vaс╕е, a similar dat. *purojit├бye would have fit this vs. line. Oldenberg takes the instr. seriously and construes ├бndhasaс╕е with sut─Б╠Бya, which is certainly possible: тАЬDurch euren vor (in lokalem Sinn) ihm gewonnenen (und ihn so besch├╝tzenden) Sieg schlagt dem berauschenden Saft des ├бndhas den langz├╝ngigen Hund, ihr Freunde, hinweg.тАЭ My own interpr. instead takes ├бndhasaс╕е with pur├│jit─л: the тАЬadvance victory over the stalkтАЭ is by this interpr. the priestsтАЩ initial victory over the stalk, by pressing it for its juice, leaving it mangled and spent. This initial victory may provide the model for the violence against the encroaching dog. But I am not at all certain of this interpr.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Rather than making c a nominal main clause (with Geldner and Renou), I think it better to take the whole vs. as a rel. cl., dependent on t├бm, which opens the next vs. _________ 03
On the impossible word dur├│с╣гa(s)- see comm. ad VIII.1.13. This is the only one of its three occurrences where it qualifies soma, though in Avestan d┼лrao┼бais only used of haoma. Geldner refuses to tr. (though he discusses it extensively in n. 3a); Renou тАШdifficile ├а mouvoirтАЩ (see his n. for disc.).

The lengthened ─л╠Бof abh─л╠Бmay represent abh├н + ─л, the enclitic acc., as I suggest for the same form in the preceding hymn, IX.100.1. In fact, just as in 100.1, it could represent a degeminated ─лm before the nasal of n├бraс╕е.

Both Geldner and Renou take yaj├▒├бm as the direct obj. of hinvanti rather than the goal, as I do (Geldner тАЬSoma тАж als OpferтАЭ). It is certainly true that yaj├▒├бm тИЪhi is found elsewhere (see GeldnerтАЩs n. 3c) and that the dat. is more often used for goal or purpose with тИЪhi. Still, the sacrifice is so often the goal of motion, the goal of motion is so often in the acc., and soma is so often the obj. of тИЪhi that I prefer to keep soma and the sacrifice as separate entities.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬThe devout (worshippers) say ‘Indu flows forth for Indra’; the lord of speech (Soma) soverign of all by his might desires (our) worship.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

тАЬThe drop purifies himself for Indra,тАЭ so the gods said.
The lord of speech does battle [/seeks bounties], holding sway over all with his strength.

Jamison Brereton Notes

On the double sense of makh├б- and its denom. and deriv., see I.18.9, III.31.7.

Griffith

Indu flows on for Indra’s sake: thus have the Deities declared.
The Lord of Speech exerts himself, Ruler of all, because of might.

English translation:

тАЬThe thousand-streamed ocean, Soma the stimulator of praise, the lord of riches, the friend of Indra flows day by day.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

Possessing a thousand streams he purifies himself, a sea setting speech aswing,
Soma, lord of riches, comrade of Indra, day after day.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The phrase samudr├│ v─Бcam─лс╣Еkhay├бс╕е recalls the variant compds in IX.35.2 and 5: samudram─лс╣Еkhaya (2a) and v─Бcam─лс╣Еkhay├бm (5a).

Note that all 4 p─Бdas begin with s-.

English translation:

тАЬThis Soma being purified hastens (to the pitcher) fostering, wealth-giving, to be enjoyed (by all); the lord of all beings, he has illuminated both heaven and earth.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

This one here is P┼лс╣гan, Wealth, Fortune; Soma rushes as he is being purified.
As lord of all creation, he has surveyed both world-halves.

Jamison Brereton Notes

bh┼л╠Бman- generally means тАШearthтАЩ (as opposed to heaven) or тАШworldтАЩ. Here it seems a little outside its usual patch, as a metrical driven variant of the common phrase v├н┼Ыvasya bh├║vanasya тАЬof all creationтАЭ; see esp. IX.86.5 p├бtir v├н┼Ыvasya bh├║vanasya r─Бjasi matching our p├бtir v├н┼Ыvasya bh┼л╠Бmanaс╕е, but also occurrences in I.164.21, II.27.4, 40.1, III.46.2, V.85.3, IX.86.28, 36, 97.56, X.45.6, 168.2, all but one straddling a late caesura, where the two light init. syllables of bhuvanasya fit well; bh├║vanasya of course fits the cadence of no Vedic meter.

English translation:

тАЬThe affectionate emulous cows celebrate (Soma) for his exhilaration; the brilliant filtered (Soma) juices make paths (for themselves to flow).тАЭ

The soma drinks make their own paths, the self-purifying drops. с│У

English translation:

тАЬBring to us, filtered (Soma), that celebrated (beverage) which is most potent, which (waits) upon the fiver orders of beings and by which we may obtain riches.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

The one who is strongest [=Indra], bring him here, o self-purifying one, the one worthy of fame,
who sur(mounts) the five domains, with whom we shall gain wealth.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Both Geldner and Renou supply тАШwealthтАЩ (ray├нm), found in d, as the referent of all the previous nom. and acc. forms. I think rather of Indra. The splv. ├│jiс╣гс╣нha- regularly modifies Indra (and never wealth). Though Geldner is correct (n. 9a) that ┼Ыrav─Б╠Бyya- is a тАЬbeliebtes BeiwortтАЭ of wealth, Indra is hardly unworthy of fame, and see V.86.2b, where du. ┼Ыrav─Б╠Бyy─Б characterizes Indra and Agni. In the same vs. (V.86.2c) both gods are described as y─Б╠Бp├б├▒ca carс╣гan─л╠Бr abh├н (though variants of this p─Бda are usually applied to Agni alone: IV.7.4=V.23.1, VII.15.2), exactly like our c save for the number of the rel. prn. The clinching arg. against ray├н- as the referent seems to me to be p─Бda d. Geldner and Renou clearly take the rel. cl. there as consisting only of y├йna v├бn─Бmahai, with p─Бda-init. acc. ray├нm part of the main cl.: the referent for t├бm back in p─Бda a and the antecedent of immed. flg. y├йna in its own p─Бda. But this would be an unusual syntactic configuration for several reasons. First, thereтАЩs a rel. cl. (in c) intervening between the acc. t├бm and its distant referent in p─Бda a (and acc. modifier in b). Moreover, in a p─Бda with the structure #X REL тАж, the rel. is usually postposed and the p─Бda syntactically self-contained - that is, the X is part of the rel. cl. The type of intra-p─Бda clausal break envisioned by Geldner/Renou is rare. Moreover, Geldner and Renou are required to interpr. v├бn─Бmahai in absolute usage (тАЬтАж wir Sieger werdenтАЭ; тАЬnous serons vainqueursтАЭ), but тИЪvan ordinarily takes a direct object, on occasion, in fact, ray├нm (e.g., VI.38.1). For all these reasons I think itтАЩs clear that ray├нm and y├йna in d cannot be coreferential and we need a different referent for the t├бm and y├б- forms - with Indra the most obvious one, for the reasons just given.

In c I supply a form of тИЪas with abh├н in the meaning тАШsurmount, dominate, prevail overтАЩ.

English translation:

тАЬEffused by the stones, recognized upon the cowhide acquirers of wealth they bestow food upon us from all sides.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

They bestow food upon us: samasvaran = liс╣н, they sounded food for us

Jamison Brereton

Having been pressed by the stones, conspicuous on the hide of the cow, the finders of goods have roared together from all around toward refreshment for us.

Jamison Brereton Notes

This vs. shows a few minor disturbances. To begin with, the employment of the preverb / particle v├н is unclear. Grassmann takes it with the pf. part. suс╣гv─Бn─Б╠Бsaс╕е, which it immed. follows, but тИЪsu is not otherwise found with v├н. Moreover, tmesis of preverbs with participles is fairly rare, though at least here the two forms are adjacent. Renou construes it with the aor. part. c├нt─Бn─Бс╕е in b; here the problem is opposite: v├н тИЪcit is indeed an idiom, but not only is tmesis in participles rare, but the position of v├н, if itтАЩs a preverb in tmesis, would be anomalous: mid-p─Бda and not only separated from its participle, but also intrusive in a consituent: suс╣гv─Бn─Б╠Бsaс╕е тАж ├бdribhiс╕е тАЬhaving been pressed by stones.тАЭ I donтАЩt have a real solution, but I wonder if itтАЩs meant to evoke the тАЬthrough/across the sheepтАЩs fleeceтАЭ expression, found, e.g., in nearby IX.100.4 тАж vy ├аvy├бyam (cf. also IX.13.6, 49.4, 61.17, 67.5, 85.5, 97.56, 109.16). It is also possible that v├н .. c├нt─Бn─Бс╕е somehow anticipates vipa┼Ыc├нtaс╕е in the next vs. (12a), but this seems a long shot.

The part. c├нt─Бna- is another bit of a problem. It is the only form to this part., which seems to belong to a root aor. otherwise found mostly in the well-attested pass.

aor. ├бceti / c├йti. The semantics works fine, but for a root aor. part. its root accent is anomalous (expect *cit─Бn├б-), and in fact a root-accented zero-grade is peculiar whatever the formation. It could of course have voc. accent - but thereтАЩs no place for a voc. in this 3rd ps. context. Perhaps it received its root accent redactionally in imitation of vipa┼Ыc├нtaс╕е in 12a.

Both Geldner and Renou take the verbal idiom in cd as transitive, or at least construe iс╣г├бm as a species of Inhaltsakk. (e.g., тАЬnous ont en r├йsonnant assembl├й de toutes parts la jouissance-rituelleтАЭ). But RenouтАЩs invocation of the idiom abh├н тАж s├бm тИЪsvar as the basis for our abh├нtaс╕е, s├бm asvaran seems quite apposite, and that idiom is intrans.

with an acc. of goal. See, e.g., IX.110.8 ├нndram abh├н тАж s├бm asvaran тАЬ They cried out in unison towards IndraтАЭ (sim. IX.106.11, 67.9). The conversion of the preverb abh├н into the adverbial abh├нtaс╕е would not be responsible for transitivizing the idiom. Cf., e.g., X.27.8 h├бv─Б ├нd ary├│ abh├нtaс╕е s├бm ─Бyan тАЬThe cries of the Stranger came together from all sides.тАЭ I agree that ├нс╣гam is an unexpected goal for this idiom, but I think we have to live with it.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬLet no mortal hear the sound of the effused Soma; drive off the dog that sacrifices not as the Bhс╣Ыgus drove off Makha.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Let no mortal: ‘No death-giving, i.e., rite-obstructing dog’;

Ar─Бdhasam = stingy, r─Бdhakakarmarahitam

Jamison Brereton

Like a mortal he [=the dog] has shown preference for the speech of the stalk being pressed.
Smite away the dog, the ungenerous one, as the Bhr╠еgus (did) the Battler.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Geldner takes n├б in b as the neg. (flg. S─Бyaс╣Зa.) and asserts (n. 13), contra Oldenberg, that the presence of the mortal and the dog in this vs. (and the VS vs. Oldenberg cites) is an accident. But the position of n├б in the p─Бda is that of the simile particle (though at least it would immed. precede the verb), and the wealth of similes in this tс╣Ыca supports a simile reading here as well. The point of the hemistich is that, like the human, the dog is attracted to the sound of the soma ritual, particularly the sound of the soma pressing, and invades it.

The 2nd hemistich begins like 1c: ├бpa ┼Ыv─Б╠Бnam тАж The simile in d, тАЬas the Bhr╠еgus did the BattlerтАЭ (makh├бс╣Г n├б bhс╣Ы╠Бgavaс╕е), refers to what Geldner calls an otherwise unknown saga. The makh├б- is found as a defeated enemy of Indra in X.171.2, a hymn attributed to one Iс╣нa Bh─Бrgava, the patronymic of the victors in our vs.. Note that the denom. verb makhasyate occurs in our vs. 5c.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The dog returns from the first tс╣Ыca in the first vs. of this one. The tс╣Ыca is also more rhetorically ambitious than those in the middle of this hymn, with an abundance of similes (13b, d, 14b, c, d, 15b). Vss. 14 and 15 end identically.

Griffith

Let not the power of men restrain the voice of the outpouring juice:
As Bhrgu’s sons chased Makha, so drive ye the greedy hound away.

Geldner

Dieser Rede des ausgepre├Яten Safts hat der Sterbliche nicht den Vorzug gegeben. Schlaget den knickerigen Hund fort wie die Bhrigu┬┤s den Makha!

Der Gott erw├дhl’ sich wie ein Mensch dies Lied des ausgepressten Tranks; Den gabenlosen Hund verjagt, gleichwie der Bhrigustamm den Feind.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬ(Soma) the kinsman (of the gods) is enveloped in the investing filter like a child in the arms of its protecting parents; he hastens like a gallant to a mistress, like a bridegroom (to the bride) to sit upon his station (the pitcher).тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

Their kinsman has wrapped himself in a cloak (of milk) to enjoy it, like a son in the two arms (of his mother).
He runs like a swain to a maiden, like a wooer, to sit in the womb.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The d p─Бda (var├│) n├б y├│nim ─Бs├бdam is almost identical to 15d (vedh─Б╠Б) n├б y├│nim ─Бs├бdam, both expressing the endpoint of SomaтАЩs ritual journey.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The dog returns from the first tс╣Ыca in the first vs. of this one. The tс╣Ыca is also more rhetorically ambitious than those in the middle of this hymn, with an abundance of similes (13b, d, 14b, c, d, 15b). Vss. 14 and 15 end identically.

Griffith

The Friend hath wrapped him in his robe, as in his parents arms, a son.
He went, as lover to a dame, to take his station suitor-like.

Geldner

Der Verwandte hat sich in sein Gewand geh├╝llt, wie der Sohn in die Arme der Mutter, um die Milch zu genie├Яen. Er eilt wie der Buhle zur Frau, wie der Liebhaber, um sich auf seinen Platz, den Scho├Я, zu setzen.

Grassmann

Der Freund hat sich ins Kleid geh├╝llt, wie an die Mutterbrust das Kind; Er eilt wie zu der Braut der Mann, zu sitzen in der Liebsten, Schooss.

English translation:

тАЬThe green-tinted (Soma), the implement of strength, the hero, who has upheld heaven and earth, is enveloped in the filter like the sacrificer (in his house) to sit upon his station.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

He is a hero, bringing skill to realization, who has propped apart the two world-halves.
The tawny one has wrapped himself in the filter, like a ritual adept, to sit in the womb.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The isolated summary vs. The cowтАЩs hide (g├бvye ├бdhi tvac├н) closely matches g├│r ├бdhi tvac├н in 11b, but the sheepтАЩs fleece is found nowhere else in the hymn, unless the v├н of 11a gestures towards it. See comm. ad loc.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The dog returns from the first tс╣Ыca in the first vs. of this one. The tс╣Ыca is also more rhetorically ambitious than those in the middle of this hymn, with an abundance of similes (13b, d, 14b, c, d, 15b). Vss. 14 and 15 end identically.

Griffith

That Hero who produces strength, he who hath propped both worlds apart,
Gold-hued, hath wrapped him in the sieve, to settle, priest-like, in his place.

рдкрджрд╛рд░реНрдерд╛рдиреНрд╡рдпрднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдГ - (рд╣рд░рд┐рдГ) рдЙрдХреНрдд рдкрд░рдорд╛рддреНрдорд╛ (рдЗрдиреНрджреНрд░рд╕реНрдп) рдХрд░реНрдордпреЛрдЧреА рдХреЗ (рдирд┐рд╖реНрдХреГрддрдореН) рд╕рджреНрдЧреБрдгрд╕рдореНрдкрдиреНрди рдЕрдиреНрддрдГрдХрд░рдг рдХреЛ (рдЕрднреНрдпреЗрддрд┐) рдкреНрд░рд╛рдкреНрдд рд╣реЛрддрд╛ рд╣реИ, (рд╡реГрд╖рд╛) рд╡рд╣ рд╕рдм рдХрд╛рдордирд╛рдУрдВ рдХреА рд╡рд░реНрд╖рд╛ рдХрд░рдиреЗрд╡рд╛рд▓рд╛ (рдЧрд╡реНрдпреЗ рдЕрдзрд┐рддреНрд╡рдЪрд┐) рдЗрдиреНрджреНрд░рд┐рдпреЛрдВ рдХреЗ рдЕрдзрд┐рд╖реНрдард╛рддрд╛ рдорди рдореЗрдВ рд╕реНрдерд┐рд░ рд╣реЛрдХрд░ (рдХрдирд┐рдХреНрд░рджрддреН) рдЧрд░реНрдЬрддрд╛ рд╣реБрдЖ (рдкрд╡рддреЗ) рд░рдХреНрд╖рд╛ рдХрд░рддрд╛ рд╣реИ, (рд╕реЛрдордГ) рд╡рд╣ рд╕рд░реНрд╡реЛрддреНрдкрд╛рджрдХ рдкрд░рдорд╛рддреНрдорд╛ (рдЕрд╡реНрдпрдГ) рдЬреЛ рд╕рд░реНрд╡рд░рдХреНрд╖рдХ рд╣реИ, рд╡рд╣ (рд╡рд╛рд░реЗрднрд┐рдГ) рдкрд╡рд┐рддреНрд░ рд╕рджреНрднрд╛рд╡реЛрдВ рд╕реЗ рд╕рдиреНрдорд╛рд░реНрдЧрд╛рдиреБрдпрд╛рдпрд┐рдпреЛрдВ рдХреА рд░рдХреНрд╖рд╛ рдХрд░рддрд╛ рд╣реИреерезремрее

рдЖрд░реНрдпрдореБрдирд┐ - рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдердГ

рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдерднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдГ - рдпрд╣рд╛рдБ рдХрдИ рдПрдХ рд▓реЛрдЧ (рдЧрд╡реНрдпреЗ рдЕрдзрд┐рддреНрд╡рдЪрд┐) рдХреЗ рдЕрд░реНрде рдЧреЛрдЪрд░реНрдо рдХреЗ рдХрд░рддреЗ рд╣реИрдВ, рдРрд╕рд╛ рдХрд░рдирд╛ рд╡реЗрдж рдХреЗ рдЖрд╢рдп рд╕реЗ рд╕рд░реНрд╡рдерд╛ рд╡рд┐рд░реБрджреНрдз рд╣реИред рди рдХреЗрд╡рд▓ рд╡реЗрджрд╛рд╢рдп рд╕реЗ рд╡рд┐рд░реБрджреНрдз рд╣реИ, рдХрд┐рдиреНрддреБ рдкреНрд░рд╕рд┐рджреНрдзрд┐ рд╕реЗ рднреА рд╡рд┐рд░реБрджреНрдз рд╣реИ, рдХреНрдпреЛрдВрдХрд┐ рдЕрдзрд┐рддреНрд╡рдЪрд┐ рдХреЗ рдЕрд░реНрде рдЧреЛрдЪрд░реНрдо рдкрд░ рдЧрд░реНрдЬрдирд╛ рдХрд┐рдпреЗ рдЧрдпреЗ рд╣реИрдВ рдФрд░ рдЧреЛрдЪрд░реНрдо рдкрд░ рдЧрд░реНрдЬрдирд╛ рдЕрдиреБрднрд╡ рд╕реЗ рд╕рд░реНрд╡рдерд╛ рд╡рд┐рд░реБрджреНрдз рд╣реИред рдЗрд╕ рдЕрдзрд┐рддреНрд╡рдЪрд┐ рдХреЗ рдЕрд░реНрде рдордирд░реВрдк рдЕрдзрд┐рд╖реНрдард╛рддрд╛ рдХреЗ рд╣реА рдареАрдХ рд╣реИрдВ, рдХрд┐рд╕реА рдЕрдиреНрдп рд╡рд╕реНрддреБ рдХреЗ рдирд╣реАрдВреерезремрее рдпрд╣ резрежрез рд╡рд╛рдБ рд╕реВрдХреНрдд рдФрд░ рддреАрд╕рд░рд╛ рд╡рд░реНрдЧ рд╕рдорд╛рдкреНрдд рд╣реБрдЖрее

рдЖрд░реНрдпрдореБрдирд┐ - рдкрджрд╛рд░реНрдердГ

рдкрджрд╛рд░реНрдерд╛рдиреНрд╡рдпрднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдГ - (рд╣рд░рд┐рдГ) рдкрд░рдорд╛рддреНрдорд╛ (рдЗрдиреНрджреНрд░рд╕реНрдп) рдХрд░реНрдордпреЛрдЧрд┐рдирдГ (рдирд┐рд╖реНрдХреГрддрдВ) рд╕рдВрд╕реНрдХреГрддрд╛рдиреНрддрдГрдХрд░рдгрдореН (рдЕрднрд┐ рдПрддрд┐) рдЖрдкреНрдиреЛрддрд┐ (рд╡реГрд╖рд╛) рд╕рд░реНрд╡рдХрд╛рдордкреНрд░рджрдГ рд╕рдГ (рдЧрд╡реНрдпреЗ, рдЕрдзрд┐, рддреНрд╡рдЪрд┐) рдЗрдиреНрджреНрд░рд┐рдпрд╛рдзрд┐рд╖реНрдард╛рддрд░рд┐ рдордирд╕рд┐ рд╕реНрдерд┐рд░реЛ рднреВрддреНрд╡рд╛ (рдХрдирд┐рдХреНрд░рджрддреН) рдЧрд░реНрдЬрдиреН (рдкрд╡рддреЗ) рд░рдХреНрд╖рддрд┐ (рдЕрд╡реНрдпрдГ) рд░рдХреНрд╖рдХрдГ (рд╕реЛрдордГ) рдкрд░рдорд╛рддреНрдорд╛ (рд╡рд╛рд░реЗрднрд┐рдГ) рдкрд╡рд┐рддреНрд░рднрд╛рд╡реИрдГ рд╕реНрд╡рднрдХреНрддрд╛рдиреН рд░рдХреНрд╖рддрд┐реерезремрее рдЗрддреНрдпреЗрдХрд╢рддрддрдордВ рд╕реВрдХреНрддрдВ рддреГрддреАрдпреЛ рд╡рд░реНрдЧрд╢реНрдЪ рд╕рдорд╛рдкреНрддрдГрее
